  69 /**
  70  * This class consists exclusively of static methods that operate on or return
  71  * method handles. They fall into several categories:
  72  * <ul>
  73  * <li>Lookup methods which help create method handles for methods and fields.
  74  * <li>Combinator methods, which combine or transform pre-existing method handles into new ones.
  75  * <li>Other factory methods to create method handles that emulate other common JVM operations or control flow patterns.
  76  * </ul>
  77  * A lookup, combinator, or factory method will fail and throw an
  78  * {@code IllegalArgumentException} if the created method handle's type
  79  * would have <a href="MethodHandle.html#maxarity">too many parameters</a>.
  80  *
  81  * @author John Rose, JSR 292 EG
  82  * @since 1.7
  83  */
  84 public class MethodHandles {
  85 
  86     private MethodHandles() { }  // do not instantiate
  87 
  88     static final MemberName.Factory IMPL_NAMES = MemberName.getFactory();
  89 
  90     // See IMPL_LOOKUP below.
  91 
  92     //// Method handle creation from ordinary methods.
  93 
  94     /**
  95      * Returns a {@link Lookup lookup object} with
  96      * full capabilities to emulate all supported bytecode behaviors of the caller.
  97      * These capabilities include {@linkplain Lookup#hasFullPrivilegeAccess() full privilege access} to the caller.
  98      * Factory methods on the lookup object can create
  99      * <a href="MethodHandleInfo.html#directmh">direct method handles</a>
 100      * for any member that the caller has access to via bytecodes,
 101      * including protected and private fields and methods.
 102      * This lookup object is a <em>capability</em> which may be delegated to trusted agents.
 103      * Do not store it in place where untrusted code can access it.
 104      * <p>
 105      * This method is caller sensitive, which means that it may return different
 106      * values to different callers.
 107      * @return a lookup object for the caller of this method, with
 108      * {@linkplain Lookup#hasFullPrivilegeAccess() full privilege access}
 109      */
 110     @CallerSensitive
 111     @ForceInline // to ensure Reflection.getCallerClass optimization
 112     public static Lookup lookup() {
 113         return new Lookup(Reflection.getCallerClass());
 114     }
 115 
 116     /**
 117      * This reflected$lookup method is the alternate implementation of
 118      * the lookup method when being invoked by reflection.
 119      */
 120     @CallerSensitive
 121     private static Lookup reflected$lookup() {
 122         Class<?> caller = Reflection.getCallerClass();
 123         if (caller.getClassLoader() == null) {
 124             throw newIllegalArgumentException("illegal lookupClass: "+caller);
 125         }
 126         return new Lookup(caller);
 127     }
 128 
 129     /**
 130      * Returns a {@link Lookup lookup object} which is trusted minimally.
 131      * The lookup has the {@code UNCONDITIONAL} mode.
 132      * It can only be used to create method handles to public members of
 133      * public classes in packages that are exported unconditionally.
 134      * <p>
 135      * As a matter of pure convention, the {@linkplain Lookup#lookupClass() lookup class}
 136      * of this lookup object will be {@link java.lang.Object}.
 137      *
 138      * @apiNote The use of Object is conventional, and because the lookup modes are
 139      * limited, there is no special access provided to the internals of Object, its package
 140      * or its module.  This public lookup object or other lookup object with
 141      * {@code UNCONDITIONAL} mode assumes readability. Consequently, the lookup class
 142      * is not used to determine the lookup context.
 143      *
 144      * <p style="font-size:smaller;">
 145      * <em>Discussion:</em>
 146      * The lookup class can be changed to any other class {@code C} using an expression of the form
 147      * {@link Lookup#in publicLookup().in(C.class)}.
 148      * A public lookup object is always subject to
 149      * <a href="MethodHandles.Lookup.html#secmgr">security manager checks</a>.
 150      * Also, it cannot access
 151      * <a href="MethodHandles.Lookup.html#callsens">caller sensitive methods</a>.
 152      * @return a lookup object which is trusted minimally
 153      *
 154      * @revised 9
 155      * @spec JPMS
 156      */
 157     public static Lookup publicLookup() {
 158         return Lookup.PUBLIC_LOOKUP;
 159     }
 160 
 161     /**
 162      * Returns a {@link Lookup lookup} object on a target class to emulate all supported
 163      * bytecode behaviors, including <a href="MethodHandles.Lookup.html#privacc">private access</a>.
 164      * The returned lookup object can provide access to classes in modules and packages,
 165      * and members of those classes, outside the normal rules of Java access control,
 166      * instead conforming to the more permissive rules for modular <em>deep reflection</em>.
 167      * <p>
 168      * A caller, specified as a {@code Lookup} object, in module {@code M1} is
 169      * allowed to do deep reflection on module {@code M2} and package of the target class
 170      * if and only if all of the following conditions are {@code true}:
 171      * <ul>
 172      * <li>If there is a security manager, its {@code checkPermission} method is
 173      * called to check {@code ReflectPermission("suppressAccessChecks")} and
 174      * that must return normally.
 175      * <li>The caller lookup object must have {@linkplain Lookup#hasFullPrivilegeAccess()
 176      * full privilege access}.  Specifically:
 177      *   <ul>
 178      *     <li>The caller lookup object must have the {@link Lookup#MODULE MODULE} lookup mode.
 179      *         (This is because otherwise there would be no way to ensure the original lookup
 180      *         creator was a member of any particular module, and so any subsequent checks
 181      *         for readability and qualified exports would become ineffective.)
 182      *     <li>The caller lookup object must have {@link Lookup#PRIVATE PRIVATE} access.
 183      *         (This is because an application intending to share intra-module access
 184      *         using {@link Lookup#MODULE MODULE} alone will inadvertently also share
 185      *         deep reflection to its own module.)
 186      *   </ul>
 187      * <li>The target class must be a proper class, not a primitive or array class.
 188      * (Thus, {@code M2} is well-defined.)
 189      * <li>If the caller module {@code M1} differs from
 190      * the target module {@code M2} then both of the following must be true:
 191      *   <ul>
 192      *     <li>{@code M1} {@link Module#canRead reads} {@code M2}.</li>
 193      *     <li>{@code M2} {@link Module#isOpen(String,Module) opens} the package
 194      *         containing the target class to at least {@code M1}.</li>
 195      *   </ul>
 196      * </ul>
 197      * <p>
 198      * If any of the above checks is violated, this method fails with an
 199      * exception.
 200      * <p>
 201      * Otherwise, if {@code M1} and {@code M2} are the same module, this method
 202      * returns a {@code Lookup} on {@code targetClass} with
 203      * {@linkplain Lookup#hasFullPrivilegeAccess() full privilege access} and
 204      * {@code null} previous lookup class.
 205      * <p>
 206      * Otherwise, {@code M1} and {@code M2} are two different modules.  This method
 207      * returns a {@code Lookup} on {@code targetClass} that records
 208      * the lookup class of the caller as the new previous lookup class and
 209      * drops {@code MODULE} access from the full privilege access.
 210      *
 211      * @param targetClass the target class
 212      * @param caller the caller lookup object
 213      * @return a lookup object for the target class, with private access
 214      * @throws IllegalArgumentException if {@code targetClass} is a primitive type or void or array class
 215      * @throws NullPointerException if {@code targetClass} or {@code caller} is {@code null}
 216      * @throws SecurityException if denied by the security manager
 217      * @throws IllegalAccessException if any of the other access checks specified above fails
 218      * @since 9
 219      * @spec JPMS
 220      * @see Lookup#dropLookupMode
 221      * @see <a href="MethodHandles.Lookup.html#cross-module-lookup">Cross-module lookups</a>
 222      */
 223     public static Lookup privateLookupIn(Class<?> targetClass, Lookup caller) throws IllegalAccessException {
 224         if (caller.allowedModes == Lookup.TRUSTED) {
 225             return new Lookup(targetClass);
 226         }
 227 
 228         SecurityManager sm = System.getSecurityManager();
 229         if (sm != null) sm.checkPermission(ACCESS_PERMISSION);
 230         if (targetClass.isPrimitive())
 231             throw new IllegalArgumentException(targetClass + " is a primitive class");
 232         if (targetClass.isArray())
 233             throw new IllegalArgumentException(targetClass + " is an array class");
 234         // Ensure that we can reason accurately about private and module access.
 235         if (!caller.hasFullPrivilegeAccess())
 236             throw new IllegalAccessException("caller does not have PRIVATE and MODULE lookup mode");
 237 
 238         // previous lookup class is never set if it has MODULE access
 239         assert caller.previousLookupClass() == null;
 240 
 241         Class<?> callerClass = caller.lookupClass();
 242         Module callerModule = callerClass.getModule();  // M1
 243         Module targetModule = targetClass.getModule();  // M2
 244         Class<?> newPreviousClass = null;
 245         int newModes = Lookup.FULL_POWER_MODES;
 246 
 247         if (targetModule != callerModule) {
 248             if (!callerModule.canRead(targetModule))
 249                 throw new IllegalAccessException(callerModule + " does not read " + targetModule);
 250             if (targetModule.isNamed()) {
 251                 String pn = targetClass.getPackageName();
 252                 assert !pn.isEmpty() : "unnamed package cannot be in named module";
 253                 if (!targetModule.isOpen(pn, callerModule))
 254                     throw new IllegalAccessException(targetModule + " does not open " + pn + " to " + callerModule);
 255             }
 256 
 257             // M2 != M1, set previous lookup class to M1 and drop MODULE access
 258             newPreviousClass = callerClass;
 259             newModes &= ~Lookup.MODULE;
 260 
 261             if (!callerModule.isNamed() && targetModule.isNamed()) {
 262                 IllegalAccessLogger logger = IllegalAccessLogger.illegalAccessLogger();
 263                 if (logger != null) {
 264                     logger.logIfOpenedForIllegalAccess(caller, targetClass);
 265                 }
 266             }
 267         }
 268         return Lookup.newLookup(targetClass, newPreviousClass, newModes);
 269     }
 270 
 271     /**
 272      * Returns the <em>class data</em> associated with the lookup class
 273      * of the specified {@code Lookup} object, or {@code null}.
 274      *
 275      * <p> Classes can be created with class data by calling
 276      * {@link Lookup#defineHiddenClassWithClassData(byte[], Object, Lookup.ClassOption...)
 277      * Lookup::defineHiddenClassWithClassData}.
 278      * A hidden class with a class data behaves as if the hidden class
 279      * has a private static final unnamed field pre-initialized with
 280      * the class data and this method is equivalent as if calling
 281      * {@link ConstantBootstraps#getStaticFinal(Lookup, String, Class)} to
 282      * obtain the value of such field corresponding to the class data.
 283      *
 284      * <p> The {@linkplain Lookup#lookupModes() lookup modes} for this lookup
 285      * must have {@link Lookup#ORIGINAL ORIGINAL} access in order to retrieve
 286      * the class data.
 287      *
 288      * @apiNote
 289      * This method can be called as a bootstrap method for a dynamically computed
 290      * constant.  A framework can create a hidden class with class data, for
 291      * example that can be {@code List.of(o1, o2, o3....)} containing more than
 292      * one live object.  The class data is accessible only to the lookup object
 293      * created by the original caller but inaccessible to other members
 294      * in the same nest.  If a framework passes security sensitive live objects
 295      * to a hidden class via class data, it is recommended to load the value
 296      * of class data as a dynamically computed constant instead of storing
 297      * the live objects in private fields which are accessible to other
 298      * nestmates.
 299      *
 300      * @param <T> the type to cast the class data object to
 301      * @param caller the lookup context describing the class performing the
 302      * operation (normally stacked by the JVM)
 303      * @param name ignored
 304      * @param type the type of the class data
 305      * @return the value of the class data if present in the lookup class;
 306      * otherwise {@code null}
 307      * @throws IllegalAccessException if the lookup context does not have
 308      * original caller access
 309      * @throws ClassCastException if the class data cannot be converted to
 310      * the specified {@code type}
 311      * @see Lookup#defineHiddenClassWithClassData(byte[], Object, Lookup.ClassOption...)
 312      * @since 15
 313      */
 314     static <T> T classData(Lookup caller, String name, Class<T> type) throws IllegalAccessException {
 315         if (!caller.hasFullPrivilegeAccess()) {
 316             throw new IllegalAccessException(caller + " does not have full privilege access");
 317         }
 318         Object classData = MethodHandleNatives.classData(caller.lookupClass);
 319         return type.cast(classData);
 320     }
 321 
 322     /**
 323      * Performs an unchecked "crack" of a
 324      * <a href="MethodHandleInfo.html#directmh">direct method handle</a>.
 325      * The result is as if the user had obtained a lookup object capable enough
 326      * to crack the target method handle, called
 327      * {@link java.lang.invoke.MethodHandles.Lookup#revealDirect Lookup.revealDirect}
 328      * on the target to obtain its symbolic reference, and then called
 329      * {@link java.lang.invoke.MethodHandleInfo#reflectAs MethodHandleInfo.reflectAs}
 330      * to resolve the symbolic reference to a member.
 331      * <p>
 332      * If there is a security manager, its {@code checkPermission} method
 333      * is called with a {@code ReflectPermission("suppressAccessChecks")} permission.
 334      * @param <T> the desired type of the result, either {@link Member} or a subtype
 335      * @param target a direct method handle to crack into symbolic reference components
 336      * @param expected a class object representing the desired result type {@code T}
 337      * @return a reference to the method, constructor, or field object
 338      * @throws    SecurityException if the caller is not privileged to call {@code setAccessible}
 339      * @throws    NullPointerException if either argument is {@code null}
 340      * @throws    IllegalArgumentException if the target is not a direct method handle
 341      * @throws    ClassCastException if the member is not of the expected type
 342      * @since 1.8
 343      */
 344     public static <T extends Member> T reflectAs(Class<T> expected, MethodHandle target) {
 345         SecurityManager smgr = System.getSecurityManager();
 346         if (smgr != null)  smgr.checkPermission(ACCESS_PERMISSION);
 347         Lookup lookup = Lookup.IMPL_LOOKUP;  // use maximally privileged lookup
 348         return lookup.revealDirect(target).reflectAs(expected, lookup);
 349     }
 350     // Copied from AccessibleObject, as used by Method.setAccessible, etc.:
 351     private static final java.security.Permission ACCESS_PERMISSION =
 352         new ReflectPermission("suppressAccessChecks");
 353 
 354     /**
 355      * A <em>lookup object</em> is a factory for creating method handles,
 356      * when the creation requires access checking.
 357      * Method handles do not perform
 358      * access checks when they are called, but rather when they are created.
 359      * Therefore, method handle access
 360      * restrictions must be enforced when a method handle is created.
 361      * The caller class against which those restrictions are enforced
 362      * is known as the {@linkplain #lookupClass() lookup class}.
 363      * <p>
 364      * A lookup class which needs to create method handles will call
 365      * {@link MethodHandles#lookup() MethodHandles.lookup} to create a factory for itself.
 366      * When the {@code Lookup} factory object is created, the identity of the lookup class is
 367      * determined, and securely stored in the {@code Lookup} object.
 368      * The lookup class (or its delegates) may then use factory methods
 369      * on the {@code Lookup} object to create method handles for access-checked members.
 370      * This includes all methods, constructors, and fields which are allowed to the lookup class,
 371      * even private ones.
 372      *
 373      * <h2><a id="lookups"></a>Lookup Factory Methods</h2>
 374      * The factory methods on a {@code Lookup} object correspond to all major
 375      * use cases for methods, constructors, and fields.
 376      * Each method handle created by a factory method is the functional
 377      * equivalent of a particular <em>bytecode behavior</em>.
 378      * (Bytecode behaviors are described in section {@jvms 5.4.3.5} of
 379      * the Java Virtual Machine Specification.)
 380      * Here is a summary of the correspondence between these factory methods and
 381      * the behavior of the resulting method handles:
 382      * <table class="striped">
 383      * <caption style="display:none">lookup method behaviors</caption>
 384      * <thead>
 385      * <tr>
 386      *     <th scope="col"><a id="equiv"></a>lookup expression</th>
 387      *     <th scope="col">member</th>
 388      *     <th scope="col">bytecode behavior</th>
 389      * </tr>
 390      * </thead>
 391      * <tbody>
 392      * <tr>
 393      *     <th scope="row">{@link java.lang.invoke.MethodHandles.Lookup#findGetter lookup.findGetter(C.class,"f",FT.class)}</th>
 394      *     <td>{@code FT f;}</td><td>{@code (T) this.f;}</td>
 395      * </tr>
 396      * <tr>
 397      *     <th scope="row">{@link java.lang.invoke.MethodHandles.Lookup#findStaticGetter lookup.findStaticGetter(C.class,"f",FT.class)}</th>
 398      *     <td>{@code static}<br>{@code FT f;}</td><td>{@code (FT) C.f;}</td>
 399      * </tr>
 400      * <tr>
 401      *     <th scope="row">{@link java.lang.invoke.MethodHandles.Lookup#findSetter lookup.findSetter(C.class,"f",FT.class)}</th>
 402      *     <td>{@code FT f;}</td><td>{@code this.f = x;}</td>
 403      * </tr>
 404      * <tr>
 405      *     <th scope="row">{@link java.lang.invoke.MethodHandles.Lookup#findStaticSetter lookup.findStaticSetter(C.class,"f",FT.class)}</th>
 406      *     <td>{@code static}<br>{@code FT f;}</td><td>{@code C.f = arg;}</td>
 407      * </tr>
 408      * <tr>
 409      *     <th scope="row">{@link java.lang.invoke.MethodHandles.Lookup#findVirtual lookup.findVirtual(C.class,"m",MT)}</th>
 410      *     <td>{@code T m(A*);}</td><td>{@code (T) this.m(arg*);}</td>
 411      * </tr>
 412      * <tr>
 413      *     <th scope="row">{@link java.lang.invoke.MethodHandles.Lookup#findStatic lookup.findStatic(C.class,"m",MT)}</th>
 414      *     <td>{@code static}<br>{@code T m(A*);}</td><td>{@code (T) C.m(arg*);}</td>
 415      * </tr>
 416      * <tr>
 417      *     <th scope="row">{@link java.lang.invoke.MethodHandles.Lookup#findSpecial lookup.findSpecial(C.class,"m",MT,this.class)}</th>
 418      *     <td>{@code T m(A*);}</td><td>{@code (T) super.m(arg*);}</td>
 419      * </tr>
 420      * <tr>
 421      *     <th scope="row">{@link java.lang.invoke.MethodHandles.Lookup#findConstructor lookup.findConstructor(C.class,MT)}</th>
 422      *     <td>{@code C(A*);}</td><td>{@code new C(arg*);}</td>
 423      * </tr>
 424      * <tr>
 425      *     <th scope="row">{@link java.lang.invoke.MethodHandles.Lookup#unreflectGetter lookup.unreflectGetter(aField)}</th>
 426      *     <td>({@code static})?<br>{@code FT f;}</td><td>{@code (FT) aField.get(thisOrNull);}</td>
 427      * </tr>
 428      * <tr>
 429      *     <th scope="row">{@link java.lang.invoke.MethodHandles.Lookup#unreflectSetter lookup.unreflectSetter(aField)}</th>
 430      *     <td>({@code static})?<br>{@code FT f;}</td><td>{@code aField.set(thisOrNull, arg);}</td>
 431      * </tr>
 432      * <tr>
 433      *     <th scope="row">{@link java.lang.invoke.MethodHandles.Lookup#unreflect lookup.unreflect(aMethod)}</th>
 434      *     <td>({@code static})?<br>{@code T m(A*);}</td><td>{@code (T) aMethod.invoke(thisOrNull, arg*);}</td>
 435      * </tr>
 436      * <tr>
 437      *     <th scope="row">{@link java.lang.invoke.MethodHandles.Lookup#unreflectConstructor lookup.unreflectConstructor(aConstructor)}</th>
 438      *     <td>{@code C(A*);}</td><td>{@code (C) aConstructor.newInstance(arg*);}</td>
 439      * </tr>
 440      * <tr>
 441      *     <th scope="row">{@link java.lang.invoke.MethodHandles.Lookup#unreflectSpecial lookup.unreflectSpecial(aMethod,this.class)}</th>
 442      *     <td>{@code T m(A*);}</td><td>{@code (T) super.m(arg*);}</td>
 443      * </tr>
 444      * <tr>
 445      *     <th scope="row">{@link java.lang.invoke.MethodHandles.Lookup#findClass lookup.findClass("C")}</th>
 446      *     <td>{@code class C { ... }}</td><td>{@code C.class;}</td>
 447      * </tr>
 448      * </tbody>
 449      * </table>
 450      *
 451      * Here, the type {@code C} is the class or interface being searched for a member,
 452      * documented as a parameter named {@code refc} in the lookup methods.
 453      * The method type {@code MT} is composed from the return type {@code T}
 454      * and the sequence of argument types {@code A*}.
 455      * The constructor also has a sequence of argument types {@code A*} and
 456      * is deemed to return the newly-created object of type {@code C}.
 457      * Both {@code MT} and the field type {@code FT} are documented as a parameter named {@code type}.
 458      * The formal parameter {@code this} stands for the self-reference of type {@code C};
 459      * if it is present, it is always the leading argument to the method handle invocation.
 460      * (In the case of some {@code protected} members, {@code this} may be
 461      * restricted in type to the lookup class; see below.)
 462      * The name {@code arg} stands for all the other method handle arguments.
 463      * In the code examples for the Core Reflection API, the name {@code thisOrNull}
 464      * stands for a null reference if the accessed method or field is static,
 465      * and {@code this} otherwise.
 466      * The names {@code aMethod}, {@code aField}, and {@code aConstructor} stand
 467      * for reflective objects corresponding to the given members declared in type {@code C}.
 468      * <p>
 469      * The bytecode behavior for a {@code findClass} operation is a load of a constant class,
 470      * as if by {@code ldc CONSTANT_Class}.
 471      * The behavior is represented, not as a method handle, but directly as a {@code Class} constant.
 472      * <p>
 473      * In cases where the given member is of variable arity (i.e., a method or constructor)
 474      * the returned method handle will also be of {@linkplain MethodHandle#asVarargsCollector variable arity}.
 475      * In all other cases, the returned method handle will be of fixed arity.
 476      * <p style="font-size:smaller;">
 477      * <em>Discussion:</em>
 478      * The equivalence between looked-up method handles and underlying
 479      * class members and bytecode behaviors
 480      * can break down in a few ways:
 481      * <ul style="font-size:smaller;">
 482      * <li>If {@code C} is not symbolically accessible from the lookup class's loader,
 483      * the lookup can still succeed, even when there is no equivalent
 484      * Java expression or bytecoded constant.
 485      * <li>Likewise, if {@code T} or {@code MT}
 486      * is not symbolically accessible from the lookup class's loader,
 487      * the lookup can still succeed.
 488      * For example, lookups for {@code MethodHandle.invokeExact} and
 489      * {@code MethodHandle.invoke} will always succeed, regardless of requested type.
 490      * <li>If there is a security manager installed, it can forbid the lookup
 491      * on various grounds (<a href="MethodHandles.Lookup.html#secmgr">see below</a>).
 492      * By contrast, the {@code ldc} instruction on a {@code CONSTANT_MethodHandle}
 493      * constant is not subject to security manager checks.
 494      * <li>If the looked-up method has a
 495      * <a href="MethodHandle.html#maxarity">very large arity</a>,
 496      * the method handle creation may fail with an
 497      * {@code IllegalArgumentException}, due to the method handle type having
 498      * <a href="MethodHandle.html#maxarity">too many parameters.</a>
 499      * </ul>
 500      *
 501      * <h2><a id="access"></a>Access checking</h2>
 502      * Access checks are applied in the factory methods of {@code Lookup},
 503      * when a method handle is created.
 504      * This is a key difference from the Core Reflection API, since
 505      * {@link java.lang.reflect.Method#invoke java.lang.reflect.Method.invoke}
 506      * performs access checking against every caller, on every call.
 507      * <p>
 508      * All access checks start from a {@code Lookup} object, which
 509      * compares its recorded lookup class against all requests to
 510      * create method handles.
 511      * A single {@code Lookup} object can be used to create any number
 512      * of access-checked method handles, all checked against a single
 513      * lookup class.
 514      * <p>
 515      * A {@code Lookup} object can be shared with other trusted code,
 516      * such as a metaobject protocol.
 517      * A shared {@code Lookup} object delegates the capability
 518      * to create method handles on private members of the lookup class.
 519      * Even if privileged code uses the {@code Lookup} object,
 520      * the access checking is confined to the privileges of the
 521      * original lookup class.
 522      * <p>
 523      * A lookup can fail, because
 524      * the containing class is not accessible to the lookup class, or
 525      * because the desired class member is missing, or because the
 526      * desired class member is not accessible to the lookup class, or
 527      * because the lookup object is not trusted enough to access the member.
 528      * In the case of a field setter function on a {@code final} field,
 529      * finality enforcement is treated as a kind of access control,
 530      * and the lookup will fail, except in special cases of
 531      * {@link Lookup#unreflectSetter Lookup.unreflectSetter}.
 532      * In any of these cases, a {@code ReflectiveOperationException} will be
 533      * thrown from the attempted lookup.  The exact class will be one of
 534      * the following:
 535      * <ul>
 536      * <li>NoSuchMethodException &mdash; if a method is requested but does not exist
 537      * <li>NoSuchFieldException &mdash; if a field is requested but does not exist
 538      * <li>IllegalAccessException &mdash; if the member exists but an access check fails
 539      * </ul>
 540      * <p>
 541      * In general, the conditions under which a method handle may be
 542      * looked up for a method {@code M} are no more restrictive than the conditions
 543      * under which the lookup class could have compiled, verified, and resolved a call to {@code M}.
 544      * Where the JVM would raise exceptions like {@code NoSuchMethodError},
 545      * a method handle lookup will generally raise a corresponding
 546      * checked exception, such as {@code NoSuchMethodException}.
 547      * And the effect of invoking the method handle resulting from the lookup
 548      * is <a href="MethodHandles.Lookup.html#equiv">exactly equivalent</a>
 549      * to executing the compiled, verified, and resolved call to {@code M}.
 550      * The same point is true of fields and constructors.
 551      * <p style="font-size:smaller;">
 552      * <em>Discussion:</em>
 553      * Access checks only apply to named and reflected methods,
 554      * constructors, and fields.
 555      * Other method handle creation methods, such as
 556      * {@link MethodHandle#asType MethodHandle.asType},
 557      * do not require any access checks, and are used
 558      * independently of any {@code Lookup} object.
 559      * <p>
 560      * If the desired member is {@code protected}, the usual JVM rules apply,
 561      * including the requirement that the lookup class must either be in the
 562      * same package as the desired member, or must inherit that member.
 563      * (See the Java Virtual Machine Specification, sections {@jvms
 564      * 4.9.2}, {@jvms 5.4.3.5}, and {@jvms 6.4}.)
 565      * In addition, if the desired member is a non-static field or method
 566      * in a different package, the resulting method handle may only be applied
 567      * to objects of the lookup class or one of its subclasses.
 568      * This requirement is enforced by narrowing the type of the leading
 569      * {@code this} parameter from {@code C}
 570      * (which will necessarily be a superclass of the lookup class)
 571      * to the lookup class itself.
 572      * <p>
 573      * The JVM imposes a similar requirement on {@code invokespecial} instruction,
 574      * that the receiver argument must match both the resolved method <em>and</em>
 575      * the current class.  Again, this requirement is enforced by narrowing the
 576      * type of the leading parameter to the resulting method handle.
 577      * (See the Java Virtual Machine Specification, section {@jvms 4.10.1.9}.)
 578      * <p>
 579      * The JVM represents constructors and static initializer blocks as internal methods
 580      * with special names ({@code "<init>"} and {@code "<clinit>"}).
 581      * The internal syntax of invocation instructions allows them to refer to such internal
 582      * methods as if they were normal methods, but the JVM bytecode verifier rejects them.
 583      * A lookup of such an internal method will produce a {@code NoSuchMethodException}.
 584      * <p>
 585      * If the relationship between nested types is expressed directly through the
 586      * {@code NestHost} and {@code NestMembers} attributes
 587      * (see the Java Virtual Machine Specification, sections {@jvms
 588      * 4.7.28} and {@jvms 4.7.29}),
 589      * then the associated {@code Lookup} object provides direct access to
 590      * the lookup class and all of its nestmates
 591      * (see {@link java.lang.Class#getNestHost Class.getNestHost}).
 592      * Otherwise, access between nested classes is obtained by the Java compiler creating
 593      * a wrapper method to access a private method of another class in the same nest.
 594      * For example, a nested class {@code C.D}
 595      * can access private members within other related classes such as
 596      * {@code C}, {@code C.D.E}, or {@code C.B},
 597      * but the Java compiler may need to generate wrapper methods in
 598      * those related classes.  In such cases, a {@code Lookup} object on
 599      * {@code C.E} would be unable to access those private members.
 600      * A workaround for this limitation is the {@link Lookup#in Lookup.in} method,
 601      * which can transform a lookup on {@code C.E} into one on any of those other
 602      * classes, without special elevation of privilege.
 603      * <p>
 604      * The accesses permitted to a given lookup object may be limited,
 605      * according to its set of {@link #lookupModes lookupModes},
 606      * to a subset of members normally accessible to the lookup class.
 607      * For example, the {@link MethodHandles#publicLookup publicLookup}
 608      * method produces a lookup object which is only allowed to access
 609      * public members in public classes of exported packages.
 610      * The caller sensitive method {@link MethodHandles#lookup lookup}
 611      * produces a lookup object with full capabilities relative to
 612      * its caller class, to emulate all supported bytecode behaviors.
 613      * Also, the {@link Lookup#in Lookup.in} method may produce a lookup object
 614      * with fewer access modes than the original lookup object.
 615      *
 616      * <p style="font-size:smaller;">
 617      * <a id="privacc"></a>
 618      * <em>Discussion of private and module access:</em>
 619      * We say that a lookup has <em>private access</em>
 620      * if its {@linkplain #lookupModes lookup modes}
 621      * include the possibility of accessing {@code private} members
 622      * (which includes the private members of nestmates).
 623      * As documented in the relevant methods elsewhere,
 624      * only lookups with private access possess the following capabilities:
 625      * <ul style="font-size:smaller;">
 626      * <li>access private fields, methods, and constructors of the lookup class and its nestmates
 627      * <li>create method handles which {@link Lookup#findSpecial emulate invokespecial} instructions
 628      * <li>avoid <a href="MethodHandles.Lookup.html#secmgr">package access checks</a>
 629      *     for classes accessible to the lookup class
 630      * <li>create {@link Lookup#in delegated lookup objects} which have private access to other classes
 631      *     within the same package member
 632      * </ul>
 633      * <p style="font-size:smaller;">
 634      * Similarly, a lookup with module access ensures that the original lookup creator was
 635      * a member in the same module as the lookup class.
 636      * <p style="font-size:smaller;">
 637      * Private and module access are independently determined modes; a lookup may have
 638      * either or both or neither.  A lookup which possesses both access modes is said to
 639      * possess {@linkplain #hasFullPrivilegeAccess() full privilege access}.  Such a lookup has
 640      * the following additional capability:
 641      * <ul style="font-size:smaller;">
 642      * <li>create method handles which invoke <a href="MethodHandles.Lookup.html#callsens">caller sensitive</a> methods,
 643      *     such as {@code Class.forName}
 644      * </ul>
 645      * <p style="font-size:smaller;">
 646      * Each of these permissions is a consequence of the fact that a lookup object
 647      * with private access can be securely traced back to an originating class,
 648      * whose <a href="MethodHandles.Lookup.html#equiv">bytecode behaviors</a> and Java language access permissions
 649      * can be reliably determined and emulated by method handles.
 650      *
 651      * <h2><a id="cross-module-lookup"></a>Cross-module lookups</h2>
 652      * When a lookup class in one module {@code M1} accesses a class in another module
 653      * {@code M2}, extra access checking is performed beyond the access mode bits.
 654      * A {@code Lookup} with {@link #PUBLIC} mode and a lookup class in {@code M1}
 655      * can access public types in {@code M2} when {@code M2} is readable to {@code M1}
 656      * and when the type is in a package of {@code M2} that is exported to
 657      * at least {@code M1}.
 658      * <p>
 659      * A {@code Lookup} on {@code C} can also <em>teleport</em> to a target class
 660      * via {@link #in(Class) Lookup.in} and {@link MethodHandles#privateLookupIn(Class, Lookup)
 661      * MethodHandles.privateLookupIn} methods.
 662      * Teleporting across modules will always record the original lookup class as
 663      * the <em>{@linkplain #previousLookupClass() previous lookup class}</em>
 664      * and drops {@link Lookup#MODULE MODULE} access.
 665      * If the target class is in the same module as the lookup class {@code C},
 666      * then the target class becomes the new lookup class
 667      * and there is no change to the previous lookup class.
 668      * If the target class is in a different module from {@code M1} ({@code C}'s module),
 669      * {@code C} becomes the new previous lookup class
 670      * and the target class becomes the new lookup class.
 671      * In that case, if there was already a previous lookup class in {@code M0},
 672      * and it differs from {@code M1} and {@code M2}, then the resulting lookup
 673      * drops all privileges.
 674      * For example,
 675      * <blockquote><pre>
 676      * {@code
 677      * Lookup lookup = MethodHandles.lookup();   // in class C
 678      * Lookup lookup2 = lookup.in(D.class);
 679      * MethodHandle mh = lookup2.findStatic(E.class, "m", MT);
 680      * }</pre></blockquote>
 681      * <p>
 682      * The {@link #lookup()} factory method produces a {@code Lookup} object
 683      * with {@code null} previous lookup class.
 684      * {@link Lookup#in lookup.in(D.class)} transforms the {@code lookup} on class {@code C}
 685      * to class {@code D} without elevation of privileges.
 686      * If {@code C} and {@code D} are in the same module,
 687      * {@code lookup2} records {@code D} as the new lookup class and keeps the
 688      * same previous lookup class as the original {@code lookup}, or
 689      * {@code null} if not present.
 690      * <p>
 691      * When a {@code Lookup} teleports from a class
 692      * in one nest to another nest, {@code PRIVATE} access is dropped.
 693      * When a {@code Lookup} teleports from a class in one package to
 694      * another package, {@code PACKAGE} access is dropped.
 695      * When a {@code Lookup} teleports from a class in one module to another module,
 696      * {@code MODULE} access is dropped.
 697      * Teleporting across modules drops the ability to access non-exported classes
 698      * in both the module of the new lookup class and the module of the old lookup class
 699      * and the resulting {@code Lookup} remains only {@code PUBLIC} access.
 700      * A {@code Lookup} can teleport back and forth to a class in the module of
 701      * the lookup class and the module of the previous class lookup.
 702      * Teleporting across modules can only decrease access but cannot increase it.
 703      * Teleporting to some third module drops all accesses.
 704      * <p>
 705      * In the above example, if {@code C} and {@code D} are in different modules,
 706      * {@code lookup2} records {@code D} as its lookup class and
 707      * {@code C} as its previous lookup class and {@code lookup2} has only
 708      * {@code PUBLIC} access. {@code lookup2} can teleport to other class in
 709      * {@code C}'s module and {@code D}'s module.
 710      * If class {@code E} is in a third module, {@code lookup2.in(E.class)} creates
 711      * a {@code Lookup} on {@code E} with no access and {@code lookup2}'s lookup
 712      * class {@code D} is recorded as its previous lookup class.
 713      * <p>
 714      * Teleporting across modules restricts access to the public types that
 715      * both the lookup class and the previous lookup class can equally access
 716      * (see below).
 717      * <p>
 718      * {@link MethodHandles#privateLookupIn(Class, Lookup) MethodHandles.privateLookupIn(T.class, lookup)}
 719      * can be used to teleport a {@code lookup} from class {@code C} to class {@code T}
 720      * and create a new {@code Lookup} with <a href="#privacc">private access</a>
 721      * if the lookup class is allowed to do <em>deep reflection</em> on {@code T}.
 722      * The {@code lookup} must have {@link #MODULE} and {@link #PRIVATE} access
 723      * to call {@code privateLookupIn}.
 724      * A {@code lookup} on {@code C} in module {@code M1} is allowed to do deep reflection
 725      * on all classes in {@code M1}.  If {@code T} is in {@code M1}, {@code privateLookupIn}
 726      * produces a new {@code Lookup} on {@code T} with full capabilities.
 727      * A {@code lookup} on {@code C} is also allowed
 728      * to do deep reflection on {@code T} in another module {@code M2} if
 729      * {@code M1} reads {@code M2} and {@code M2} {@link Module#isOpen(String,Module) opens}
 730      * the package containing {@code T} to at least {@code M1}.
 731      * {@code T} becomes the new lookup class and {@code C} becomes the new previous
 732      * lookup class and {@code MODULE} access is dropped from the resulting {@code Lookup}.
 733      * The resulting {@code Lookup} can be used to do member lookup or teleport
 734      * to another lookup class by calling {@link #in Lookup::in}.  But
 735      * it cannot be used to obtain another private {@code Lookup} by calling
 736      * {@link MethodHandles#privateLookupIn(Class, Lookup) privateLookupIn}
 737      * because it has no {@code MODULE} access.
 738      *
 739      * <h2><a id="module-access-check"></a>Cross-module access checks</h2>
 740      *
 741      * A {@code Lookup} with {@link #PUBLIC} or with {@link #UNCONDITIONAL} mode
 742      * allows cross-module access. The access checking is performed with respect
 743      * to both the lookup class and the previous lookup class if present.
 744      * <p>
 745      * A {@code Lookup} with {@link #UNCONDITIONAL} mode can access public type
 746      * in all modules when the type is in a package that is {@linkplain Module#isExported(String)
 747      * exported unconditionally}.
 748      * <p>
 749      * If a {@code Lookup} on {@code LC} in {@code M1} has no previous lookup class,
 750      * the lookup with {@link #PUBLIC} mode can access all public types in modules
 751      * that are readable to {@code M1} and the type is in a package that is exported
 752      * at least to {@code M1}.
 753      * <p>
 754      * If a {@code Lookup} on {@code LC} in {@code M1} has a previous lookup class
 755      * {@code PLC} on {@code M0}, the lookup with {@link #PUBLIC} mode can access
 756      * the intersection of all public types that are accessible to {@code M1}
 757      * with all public types that are accessible to {@code M0}. {@code M0}
 758      * reads {@code M1} and hence the set of accessible types includes:
 759      *
 760      * <table class="striped">
 761      * <caption style="display:none">
 762      * Public types in the following packages are accessible to the
 763      * lookup class and the previous lookup class.
 764      * </caption>
 765      * <thead>
 766      * <tr>
 767      * <th scope="col">Equally accessible types to {@code M0} and {@code M1}</th>
 768      * </tr>
 769      * </thead>
 770      * <tbody>
 771      * <tr>
 772      * <th scope="row" style="text-align:left">unconditional-exported packages from {@code M1}</th>
 773      * </tr>
 774      * <tr>
 775      * <th scope="row" style="text-align:left">unconditional-exported packages from {@code M0} if {@code M1} reads {@code M0}</th>
 776      * </tr>
 777      * <tr>
 778      * <th scope="row" style="text-align:left">unconditional-exported packages from a third module {@code M2}
 779      * if both {@code M0} and {@code M1} read {@code M2}</th>
 780      * </tr>
 781      * <tr>
 782      * <th scope="row" style="text-align:left">qualified-exported packages from {@code M1} to {@code M0}</th>
 783      * </tr>
 784      * <tr>
 785      * <th scope="row" style="text-align:left">qualified-exported packages from {@code M0} to {@code M1}
 786      * if {@code M1} reads {@code M0}</th>
 787      * </tr>
 788      * <tr>
 789      * <th scope="row" style="text-align:left">qualified-exported packages from a third module {@code M2} to
 790      * both {@code M0} and {@code M1} if both {@code M0} and {@code M1} read {@code M2}</th>
 791      * </tr>
 792      * </tbody>
 793      * </table>
 794      *

 958      * <td>{@code privateLookupIn(C,PRI2)} fails</td>
 959      * <td></td>
 960      * <td></td>
 961      * <td></td>
 962      * <td></td>
 963      * <td style="text-align:center">IAE</td>
 964      * </tr>
 965      * <tr>
 966      * <td>{@code PRI2.in(D2)} same package</td>
 967      * <td></td>
 968      * <td></td>
 969      * <td style="text-align:center">PAC</td>
 970      * <td></td>
 971      * <td style="text-align:center">2R</td>
 972      * </tr>
 973      * <tr>

 975      * <td></td>

 980      * </tr>
 981      * <tr>

 983      * <td></td>
 984      * <td></td>

 986      * <td></td>
 987      * <td style="text-align:center">2R</td>
 988      * </tr>
 989      * <tr>
 990      * <td>{@code PRI2.in(E)} hop to third module</td>
 991      * <td></td>
 992      * <td></td>
 993      * <td></td>
 994      * <td></td>
 995      * <td style="text-align:center">none</td>

 998      * <td>{@code PRI2.dropLookupMode(PROTECTED)}</td>
 999      * <td></td>

1002      * <td></td>

1010      * <td></td>
1011      * <td style="text-align:center">2R</td>
1012      * </tr>

1014      * <td>{@code PRI2.dropLookupMode(PACKAGE)}</td>
1015      * <td></td>
1016      * <td></td>
1017      * <td></td>
1018      * <td></td>
1019      * <td style="text-align:center">2R</td>
1020      * </tr>

1023      * <td></td>
1024      * <td></td>

1026      * <td></td>
1027      * <td style="text-align:center">2R</td>
1028      * </tr>

1032      * <td></td>

1043      * <td style="text-align:center">1R</td>

1092      * </tr>

1095      * <td></td>
1096      * <td></td>

1098      * <td></td>
1099      * <td style="text-align:center">U</td>
1100      * </tr>
1101      * <tr>

1103      * <td></td>
1104      * <td></td>
